in this course we will build a real world Enterprise level patient management system using production ready microservices we will gain hands-on experience with Java spring bit and Docker for our back a microservices host grass for data storage and Kafka for event-driven communication we will also learn how to build API gateways and load balancers using spring Cloud Gateway and we will Implement both rest and grpc for microservice interaction ensuring a scalable and efficient architecture we will also learn how to authenticate users and secure our apis using authorization and Bearer tokens which we will build into a dedicated authentication Service this ensures our system follows industry security best practices Beyond development you will learn how to deploy these Services TOS using local stack write automated tests for reliability and follow best practices that are used in real world Enterprise application each module is going to build upon the last adding a new feature to our patient management system and giving you a complete endtoend understanding of how these Technologies work together in a production grade system this course is beginner friendly and we will build everything from scratch but it's not for firsttime coders so to get the most out of this course you should have some basic knowledge around Java such as variables blips classes methods and oop some basic experience with spring Boot and a simple understanding of how rest apis work some basic database knowledge and some knowledge of microservices and Docker would also be helpful so if you have built a a simple spring bit application before you are ready to take the next step into Enterprise level microservices in the first part of the course we will set up and build everything for local development we will be running our microservices and Docker using postgress SQL Docker images for our database and we will be setting up communication between our microservices using a combination of rest grpc and Kafka so this is what our development environment will look like where we will build and test each piece before moving on to deployment once development is complete things will get a bit more advanced and we will learn how to deploy everything TOS using local stack and infrastructure as code this will allow us to simulate Cloud infrastructure such as ECS RDS and API gateways as well as some of the Ws managed services like msk for Kafka all while keeping everything on our local development machine allowing us to avoid the costs associated with a real LS account by the end of this we are ready to start to create our first microservice which is going to be the patient service so before we get into building our Patient Service um I want to take a bit of time to explain the architecture of a spring boot app so spring boot is a framework that lets us build Java applications fast and efficiently and comes with a lot of nice things out of the box such as dependency management being able to build a restful API server interacting with a database and it also has a built-in server that helps us with the local development and because spring boot is modular for its dependencies that we can add as we need them it's perfect for building lightweight microservices that we can then containerize so in any spring boot architecture there are three main components or three main layers and the first one is the controller so one of the main ways that microservices can communicate with each other and also with its clients is through HTTP requests so any HTTP requests that are received to our microservice for example a get or a post request that might come from a front-end client is going to be received and handled by the controller so the controller will then do some validation for example it will check if the required properties exist in the body of the request and then it will decide what to do with that request and pass the data to the appropriate service so over here we have the service layer and the service layer does is that it handles any business logic that the request might need and it will also perform any external calls that it needs to do to fulfill that business logic so for example if we are handling the business Logic for creating a patient this might be that the email address for a patient has to be a unique email address that doesn't exist in the system already or another example would be that that patient has to live in a certain location to be able to be registered with the system so those are examples of when we say business logic and this all gets handled in the service so once the service layer has handled the business logic it will then call the repository layer for any database interactions if needed so down here we have the repository layer and the repository layer is solely responsible for interacting with the database so if we go back to our example of creating a patient when the service layer calls the repository layer the repository layer will create a new patient record in the database one of the added benefits of the repository layer is that it hides away the raw SQL queries that we would typically write when interacting with a database and allows us to interact with the database instead using what we call a model so a model in this architecture would be for example a patient so when we create a new patient we create a new patient object based on the model and then this is what gets passed to the repository and then this is what gets saved in our database so we will explain more about how this works um in the code when we see some examples as we build out our first patient service but this is essentially the architecture of our of the microservices that we are going to build so the last thing I want to talk about before we get into the code is is what's called a data transfer object or a dto so over here between the controller and the service layer you can see we are passing and returning a dto or a data transfer object sounds pretty complicated or has a complicated name but this is essentially just a simple JavaScript object which has the sole responsibility of passing data from one layer to another so so in this example where we are where we are creating a patient After we receive a post request that request will be converted to what's called a dto and this dto Java object is going to have all the properties that are received in the request in it this DTU then gets passed to the service and the service layer will convert the dto into its corresponding model entity object so for example when we're creating a patient we would have a patient request dto and in the service this will get converted to a patient entity or a patient model object before it gets saved to the database so why do we use dto there's a few reasons and the first one is that it keeps the internal domain models or in other words our database entities hidden away from the client so this this protects our app's internal structure from being returned to any of the clients it also allows us to send or receive only the fields that are relevant to the client rather than exposing all the fields in our domain model so a quick example of this would be if we have a patient domain model or a patient entity in our database this might have some private medical information in it that we do not want to expose to the front- end client so in our D we would simply ignore this field as opposed to returning it in the response so again we'll see how this looks as we build out some examples it also means we can validate the client inputs such as if the fields are valid in the request and it means we can return the errors back to the client a lot earlier as this validation is done in the controller as opposed to waiting until the request blow reaches uh the database before finding any errors so hopefully this gives you an idea of the structure of a spring Bit app in terms of what we're going to build when it comes to our Patient Service and we will refer back to this diagram as we build out the different layers of our code to help clear things up so now that we have an understanding of this what we will do is jump back into intelligy and we will create our first micros service okay so now that we have our da environment set up and we have an idea on how a spring bit application is going to look what we can do now is start to create our first micros service and this is going to be the patient service so go ahead and open up intellig idea ultimate and what you now that we have a working spring boot app that has some crud endpoints what we're going to do is introduce the concept of Docker to our application so far we have been running our Patient Service application on our development machine so either your Windows laptop or computer or your MacBook and we have been using an inmemory database for convenience but this does not replicate the production environment that we are going to create on abs and we are going to look at how Docker solves this problem so what is Docker Docker is simplifying the building and deployment of apps using lightweight containers how does this help us with the problem that we have in Enterprise environments microservices typically involve running a number of different apps software and tools so we might have two or three microservices to run we might have a reverse proxy or a load balancer and we might have a database as well as a RIS cache and so on so when it comes to development trying to install all these things is pretty timeconsuming we have to go and find the installation files for post for example and install each thing individually the other drawback here is that we can end up with different versions of the software compared to what other developers on our team would have there could be subtle differences between the versions that could cause side effects whenever it comes time to running our app so Docker allows us to simplify this process by containerizing each of these individual things follow of the config the code and the settings which each thing needs to individually run all packaged up in a nice container this means whenever a container starts and runs it's going to work the same whether it's on a specific development machine such as a Windows or Mac as well as whenever we deploy to cloud services such as Ms Azure or even Google Cloud okay so now we have an idea of what Docker does let's have a look at how it works so when we develop our apps in our ID there are typically a bunch of things and files that are needed for the app to work so we'll have some config and some dependencies so for example in our Java app we have our application properties as well as the maven dependencies that we added for the course so far we'll also have the runtime so in our course we're using Java so this is the jdk version that we need to use and then we'll obviously have our source code and this is the manual code that we added such as our endpoints our services and so on so whenever we run our code locally intellig does some smart things behind the scenes to compile our code start the runtime and deploy the config to the running code which is fine for Windows but whenever we deploy our code to WS for example we don't want to have to install the config install the runtime and run the source code every time we want to start the app on a so what we do is we create a Docker image image so a Docker image is an artifact that contains all the source code and the runtime that's needed for the app and any config and dependencies that we need for our app to work and all these things get packaged up inside this Docker image so you can think of a Docker image as a blueprint which is used to create containers so a Docker image doesn't actually start or run the app it just contains all the things you need to run the app packaged up in a single artifact that we can then use later once we have an image what we can do is we can up upload the images to a Docker image repository or a registry this is where Docker images are stored managed and distributed so this is usually stored in the cloud and there are many providers who can store Docker containers such as Docker Hub ORS ECR which is what we will be using another way to think about the docker image registry is that it's a source control repository for Docker images so just like we store all our source code on GitHub we store all our images in the docker registry for better local development Docker can also store your images in a local registry on your own development machine so on your laptop or your computer within the docker context which makes it easier and faster to test and work with containers without the overhead of having to upload the docker image to an external image repository every time you want to test a change so this is a nice feature that we'll see working as we go through the course so once we have an image created and stored in either the local Docker image repository or a cloud hosted image repository we can pull the image and start a container that is based on this image so this is an important step as this is the point where our app actually runs and it's running inside the container and this container is going to have all the config the run time and the source code package within it and once we have created a container it will run anywhere that has Docker installed so we can start this container on our local machine and using some simple Docker run commands and when it comes time to deploying the container to s we will use the same commands to run the app and this will give us confidence that our code is going to run the same regardless of the environment because all its dependencies are packaged up within the container itself as opposed to being installed individually on the machine or server that needs to run the code so hopefully this gives you an idea of how Docker works now what we'll do is we'll jump back into our code and we will create our first Docker containers okay so now we have an idea of how Docker Works what we're going to do is create our first first Docker container from an existing image so to do this we're going to set up a real postgress database for our Patient Service application so far we have been using an in-memory database for development but in production Enterprise environments you typically use a real database such as postgress so this is why we want to use a postgress database in our Dev setup as well as it gives us a production like environment to work with so I'm just in intellig here and the good thing about using intellig idea ultimate is that it has great support for working with Docker right in the ID so on the left what you want to do is go to services and just click services and you should see under services that we have an option that says Docker and if we expand out this option you can see that have an option for each of the things that Docker can use so we can see the containers that we currently don't have any running at the minute we can also see any images that we have and we can see the network and also the volumes so we'll explain a bit more about each of these as we need to and as we create images and containers and things you can see that they will appear under here this will make it really easy for us to manage things as we're developing the many microservices that we're going to need so to create and start a new Docker container what we're going to do now is up here is we're going to choose the three dots and we're going to go to edit configuration down in here and in the window that opens you'll see that we have a configuration for our patient the this plus arrow and we're going to scroll down until we see Docker here and then we're going to expand this and then we're going to choose Docker image so on the right you can see that the panel has updated and we have to enter some options so the first thing we'll do is we'll change the name for this configuration so in the name section at the the top we will change it from Docker image to say patient- service- DB because this is going to be the database for our Patient Service and as you can see over here it's updated the name so this is just to help us keep track of what our different our own image so if you go to hub.com and scroll down you can see that we have a whole bunch of outof the box images that are provided by the creators of the different tools that we can use in our own applications so these are open source and they're free to use and there's a whole bunch of stuff that we can use depending on what we want to do so if we look at databases and storage on the left here build out our microservices architecture in the same way so if you're having any problems at this point double check the logs so if you go to the services um under containers if you choose one of the containers you can see that it gives you the logs for that container and then you'll also see if there's any issues with the actual building of the container itself and from there can hopefully pinpoint the issue also have a look at the finished example on get Hub which has the finished Docker files and the environment variables that you need to run containers and things like that when you're happy we will jump into the next section so far we have been using rest to communicate with our Patient Service to send and receive data so rest is good because it has strong browser compatibility it uses Json and it works seamlessly over HTTP so in this section we're going to explore how microservices can communicate using what's called grpc so what is grpc grpc is a high performance open source RPC framework that leverages http2 and protocol buffers for efficient low latency communication between its services so that's the fancy definition but in other words while rest provides an excellent way for browsers and other clients to communicate with services using Json format grpc offers a faster and more efficient method for microservices to interact with each other utilizing the Proto buff format to send and receive data while both rest and grpc both use HTTP beneath the hood grpc is significantly faster and more efficient which is better for internal service to service communication where high performance and low latency are required so to show how this works we are going to automatically create a bilding account for a patient when a patient is created so when an admin fills out the form on the front end this form will make a rest request to the PTI service which is similar to what we've seen in our rest HTTP request when we use the intellig clients and from here the patient service will then make a grpc request with the necessary patient details which will be in proto buff format to the billing service the building service will then perform any business logic do any database stuff and anything else that it needs to do to create a building account for that patient so to work with grpc we need what's called a Proto file so this file is to describe our grpc server such as the available methods what the request looks like and what the response looks like and it's usually named based on the service that we're trying to interact with so in our case it's going to be called building service. pruto because this file describes how we interact with the building service so this file is then used to generate a grpc client and a grpc server this means we can make requests from the patient service using the grpc client which are then re received in the billing service using that grpc server so having a Proto file also means that if the server changes so for example if there are any changes to the functionality or if there are any changes in the request or response data then all of the clients are able to regenerate the code that is needed based on this new Proto file so this approach scales really well as if we add another microservice so for example down here if we added an appointment service that also needed to interact with the building service all the appointment service has to do is grab the Proto file generate the code for the client and then it is able to interact with the billing service using grpc requests to send and receive data so in the next section what we're going to do is build our billing service and we're going to build our grpc server and we're also going to add a grpc client to our existing Patient Service so that the two can interact and we'll also write our very own Proto file as well so just a quick note on the Proto file there are many ways to store a Proto file so that all the different Services can easily get access to it so there's some options here including using git subm modules or storing it as a separate mayin package which all the services would add as a dependency or we could use a cloud-based thing such as artifactory these things tend to be quite complicated to set up so for the purpose of learning to focus solely on how to use grpc we are just going to copy at the Proto file for the building server service into all of the services that need it so we're going to have one in the patient service and we'll have one in the bilding service but both these files are going to have the exact same content so just be aware of that and be aware that typically in Enterprise environments that there are more robust ways to store this file okay so now that we have an idea on how grpc Works we're going to see it in action by first building out our billing service in our project so I have the IDE open here and what we're going to do is we're going to go up to the class so that we can interact with the request and once we have completed any business logic and ready to return a response we use what's called this stream Observer so a stream Observer is a pretty powerful Concept in grpc as with a stream Observer it lets us do things like earn multiple responses to the client at the same time and also accept back and forth communication to that client so by that what I mean is once the client makes a connection to our server we can send and receive data back and forward in real time from the client to the server for example in a chat application you would send messages back back and forth and another example is with live dashboards so as we create more bilding accounts we can return the real-time billing data back to the client and they will get updates in real time and they can do what they want with those updates so this is different from standard rest HTTP requests as rest typically uses a single request single response model so in the case of rest if the client wanted to get some real-time updates on the Belling analytics for example then they would have to continuously make get requests every few seconds to our endpoint so stream observers are more powerful in that regards as it gives us a lot of flexibility into how we open connections and handle different types of back and forth communication with our clients so that's just a bit of an overview on what the stream Observer is and how we handle responses but in this example we're just going to return a single response back to the client with some information in it which have all the dependencies from the readme and have reloaded your Meen projects and things like that and otherwise if you're seeing all these log messages and getting a successful 200 response back from your create patient request then congratulations you have just created your first service to service communication functionality that uses grpc and Proto buff so at this point feel free to stop and have a look at some of the code that we just wrote and try and understand it maybe change a few things such as has add your own property to the protuff request and see what happens and when you're happy we are going to move on to the next section until this point we have been using synchronous Technologies like rest and grpc to interact with our different microservices so just to recap what this means one service sends a request to another service and then it waits for a response before it can continue execution so this is fine for any one 121 interactions but what happens when a service needs to communicate with multiple microservices at the same time so let's imagine that we have added two new microservices into our setup that need to be notified when a patient is created in a in the synchronous setup we would need to make multiple requests from our Patient Service one for each new service and then we would have to wait for a response to come back from each of these services this not only slows things down but also blocks other operations while we wait for a response this can also cause problems if our traffic grows because each time a patient is created there are going to be three internal grpc requests to the different microservices so if you imagine in an Enterprise environment where there could be hundreds or even thousands of patients being created in short succession this would increase the load on our internal servers due to the amount of internal requests that are being made now in a different scenario let's imagine where one of the services is slow or even temporarily unavailable that single bottleneck can disrupt the entire system the this is where asynchronous communication comes in so by leveraging tools like Kafka we can completely transform by our services communicate so instead of waiting on responses a service can publish an event to a CFA topic other services that then need that information can consume it independently on their own timeline and our patient service does not need to wait for a response it simply sends the events and Carries On with its current execution path so in our microservices setup we're going to send a created event using Kafka and all the services that are interested in this event in this case the analytic service and the notification service can consume these events and perform any logic it needs to perform using the data inside of the event so this method of asynchronous communication opens the door to building a more efficient and reliable system if any of our services go down in this case it doesn't affect the other services as they will continue to consume events from the topic and the patient service can still send events to the topic likewise if we need to add more microservices that perform logic when a patient is created that microservice can subscribe to the topic and consume patient created messages the patient service does not need to be updated to make requests to the new microservice so these are the new things that we are going to build and add to our current microservices setup to understand how Kafka Works in Enterprise systems to communicate between the different micros services but before we get into the code let's have a look at what some of these terms mean so that we understand better what all these different bits and pieces are doing so there's a few key parts that we need to know in order to understand Kafka and how it works so here in this green box we have What's called the Kafka broker so a Kafka broker is essentially a server that stores and delivers messages to Consumers so this is a standalone thing that will be hosted within the microservices setup depending on the setup and the architecture inside of the cfer broker we have What's called the topic so a topic is a categorized Channel where messages are stored and exchanged so basically a CFA topic is used to hold different events that match a certain category so for example we might have a CFA topic called patient events and this topic would hold all of the events that are related to a patient such as patient created events or patient updated events so an event is a single piece of data or a message that is stored on a topic and is sent from a producer so these events can have anything we want within them and are usually stored in a specific format such as Json or protuff so for example a patient created event might contain things such as the ID of the patient that was just created the name of the patient and the email address of the patient and so on so in order for events to make their way onto the CFA topic we need what's called a CF a producer so a a producer is some code that lives inside of our microservice that is used to send events or messages onto a specific cfit topic and it contains any data that any of the consumers need in order to perform the different bits of business logic that they need to do so a consumer then are also code that lives inside of individual microservices that is used to read any messages from a CFA topic so in this case we have two consumers and these would typically be two different microservices so as new microservices join they can easily subscribe to existing CFA topics as consumers or produce messages to relevant topics so any producer can send a message to any topic that it wants to and any consumer can consume a message from any topic that it wants to as well and we also see here an example of a consumer that is consuming from many topics so this architecture is really scalable as it means that any consumer can read any topic that it needs to read in order to get the data that it needs to work and any producer can send any message to any topic depending on how the system grows or the business requirements so this is the basics of CFA that we need to understand in order to get things to work there are quite a few additional features of cfad that make it really scalable and Powerful for this course we're going to focus on setting up our own CFA broker and sending messages and consuming messages from our different microservices so when do we use grpc versus Kafka grpc is perfect for one to1 communication when we need an immediate response from the other service so for example we used trpc to interact with our billing service ensuring that a patient billing account was created IM immediately when the patient was registered so CFA is ideal for one to many microservice communication when immediate responses are not required in the next section we will see this in action as we build a notification service to send a simul a simulated notification to the newly created patient and we will also blog an audit entry in our analytic service or that patient as well so container to tell it that it is the main CFA broker in the cluster of Kafka Brokers so what I mean by that is is that we can scale our Kafka servers depending on how much load our Enterprise app currently has in terms of traffic so for example we could have three CFA containers that are running in different servers and what the controller rule does is it tells this container that it is in charge of orchestrating all of the other cafka containers in our microservices setup this includes things like ensuring the topics are partitioned correctly across the different container nodes and that the messages are sent efficiently to a given CFA container without any duplication that's a brief overview of so that's just a quick overview of the controller rule and how CF can scale but the Kafka topic that about rounds it off for the Kafka sections in this part of the course so feel free to go back over some of the stuff that we have done so that you understand how producers and pieces fit together so at this point if you're having any problems make sure that you have added the correct dependencies to the analytic Service as well as the application properties that you need and have a look at the finished repository to look at the finished code to see where you might have gone wrong and don't forget to use the Kafka tool window as well to ensure that your Kafka broker is able to produce and consume messages okay so so far we have been interacting with our patient microservice directly from our client this is okay for one service but if our client needs to interact with multiple microservices it becomes a bit more complicated this is where API gateways come in so what are API gateways an API Gateway acts as a single entry point for all the clients to interact with the multiple microservices the way this works is that the API Gateway will root the request to a given microservice and the benefit of this is that it hides the internal addresses from the clients and API Gateway also means that we can handle cross cutting concerns like authentication authorization logging and monitoring rate limiting and things like caching that are common to all of the microservices so to explain a bit more about what all this means we will look at our current setup to see some of these problems and then we will see how API gateways can help us solve some of these problems so in our current setup the front end clients or the HTTP res clients will communicate directly with the patient service using rest requests this requires the client to know the exact address of the service which isn't ideal as if the address of the patient service changes so for example if the port changes or if we decide to rename this service to something else for example then all of the clients must update their configurations in order to make press request to the patient service at its new address but this leads to potential maintenance challenges depending on how many clients are consuming our patient serviced API so the other drawback to this approach is that in our Patient Service we have to expose a port so in this case it's Port 4,000 to the internet this introduces potential security risks as the service becomes directly accessible to any and all external traffic from the internet this approach also causes scalability challenges with multiple microservices as if we add more microservices such as the analytic service the client will also have to update its configuration to know the exact address of the analytic Service as well in order to perform any rest request that it needs to perform as we add more services we have to update the configuration again and this problem continues to compound as we grow our microservices application to solve some of these problems we can use what's called an API Gateway so in the middle of our screen here you can see we have a new box which represents the API Gateway and you will notice that the blow has changed slightly so now instead of our front-end client having to make press requests to each of the individual microservices all the requests in this case the request for the analytics service and a request for the patient service all go through the API gway and then the API gway will redirect a given request to the appropriate microservice so the first benefit of this is that it acts as a central entry point for all our clients so all our clients simply need to know the address of the Gateway and each time the client makes a rest request uh the Gateway will redirect the traffic which means that the frontend client only needs to know the address of the API Gateway and the API Gateway will handle the rooting to the appropriate service another benefit is that it means we can hide the internal addresses of our microservices eliminating the need for the clients to change the configuration of the endpoint should the address of each of these microservices change it also means that we no longer have to expose our Patient Service and our analytic service to the Internet by opening a port as the Gateway will handle all the external traffic this makes our microservices more secure as it's a lot harder for pckers or any Bad actors to interact with our services directly this approach also simplifies the scaling of our architecture as if and when we decide to add more microservices we only need to update the API Gateway instead of updating all of our front-end clients having an API Gateway also means we can handle authentication and authorization and a single place so an API Gateway can work with an off service so an off service would typically be a separate microservice which handles an authentication and authorization which you will see in the later parts of the course and it means that the API Gateway can pass all requests to the authorization service to make sure that the user who is making the request has access to the resource that they are trying to access so for example if the user tries to make a request to add a patient the request will get intercepted at the API Gateway point point it will get passed to the authorization service the authorization service will check if the user has access to create patience and if it does it will return a success message to the API Gateway and the API Gateway will forward the request on to the patient microservice so again we will see how this works a bit later in the off section of the course but this is just to give you an idea of some of the benefits of an API gway and the last feature to mention about API gateways is that it lets us handle other cross cutting concerns so a cross cutting concern is a feature or a piece of functionality that will affect all of the microservices regardless of what that microservice is intended to do this includes things like logging caching and bit limiting as well as authorization and authentication like we have just talked about so hopefully this gives you an idea of the benefits of an API Gateway and why we use them so in the next parts of the course we are going to use spring Cloud Gateway which lets us easily configure a new service which will act as an API Gateway in our microservices setup and let us Define the rules to WR requests and things like that okay so at this point what we can do is we can create our API Gateway project so even though this is an API Gateway and it might sound a bit different to the other services we have created so far our API gway is going to be essentially another microservice so it's going to run on Spring Boot and Docker the only difference is that we're going to use the spring Cloud Gateway dependency that comes from the spring framework so with that in mind have added direct config to the API gway run configuration and also double check the dependencies in the pom.xml as well okay so now that we have an API Gateway in place that we added to our microservices setup in the previous section what we are going to look at now is how to integrate an authentication Service into our microservices setup so authentication and authorization is quite a large Topic in itself so for this portion of the course we are going to focus on where to use Json web tokens or jwt's in order to protect the endpoints in our Patient Service microservice up until now we have been able to access our Patient Service openly from the internet regardless of who we are so when we introduce authentication what's going to happen is we are going to have to log in by making a rest request to our offs service with a username and password the off service will then take our username and password that it retrieves in a request and it will validate that those credentials are correct so I will do this by comparing the username and password combination to that it is stored in the database of the off service once the off service has verified that our credentials are correct it will then return a Json web token back to the client and this token is going to be used in the subsequent requests to prove prove who we are so by that what I mean is after the client or front end has logged in and retrieved a token it will then pass this token along with any subsequent requests to our micros Services setup so for example we will do a get request to our Patient Service which will go through the gway and we will include this D token along with that request the API gway is going to take the token and it's going to validate the token by making a call to the off service if the token is valid it will then forward the request on to the patient service and then the patient service will respond as normal if the token is invalid it will then return this result back to the API Gateway and the API Gateway will respond to the client with a 401 unauthorized status code if that happens the request does not get forwarded to the patient service it returns early and is handled by the API Gateway so there's a few steps to this so the first thing we're going to do is we're going to create a new micros service called The Off service and we will also create a database for off service in order to store the users along with any users username and password and then we will add the login inpoint that allows us to log in given our credentials and receive a JWT token so that's the first step and this is what we're going to do in the next section okay so the first step that we are going to do is we are going to create a database for our off service which is going to hold all the credentials for all of our users in our patient management app and just like we did with the patient service we are going to use a postgress database as this is a good relational database to use for our given use case so in Enterprise production environments it wouldn't matter too much what database we use here because we are using Docker and microservices so it is not uncommon to have different Technologies such as mongodb or similar for different microservices it it really depends on the use case for that microservice and how the data needs to be stored and so on but since we've used a postgress database before and a postgress database is well suited to during users we going to use a postgress database for our off service as well so with that in mind what confusing because a 500 error code typically indicates that something went wrong on the server so what we want to do is ensure that the API Gateway returns 401 any time the off service returns a 401 to the API Gateway so the reason we do this is because we want to make sure that the responses are as clear and meaningful as possible to our front-end clients because remember there are going to be a team of front-end developers who have to consume our API and if they are interacting with our API in the front end code for example and they get a 500 response back that indicates that something went wrong and it makes it more difficult for them to consume or API whereas if we send the actual status code back which informs them that the request was an offer rized this makes it easier to manage the different UI elements on the front end so to fix this we're going to create a custom exception Handler that will catch any of the 401 unauthorized errors that we receive back from our off service and we will handle them appropriately in our API Gateway okay so we will minimize the services window and we'll close down some of our files for now and we'll jump back to the API gway folder and we're course when we come to build out a simple VII for our microservices API that we're building at this point in the course we've added quite a bit of functionality and up until this point we have been testing things manually using things like intellig HTTP request client checking the logs and so on but as our system grows it takes a lot longer and sometimes becomes harder to test things manually and this is where automated testing comes in so in Enterprise environments this is a pretty important concept as it helps us validate that our code is working before it gets shipped to production testing in itself is a pretty huge concept and I can make an entire course just dedicated to testing so as part of this course what we're going to do is we are going to learn the theory behind some of the more widely used testing approaches and we will look at automating the HTTP requests we have been using so far to test our application so if we have a look um at some of the testing approaches we can use one of those is called unit testing so unit testing is testing individual units such as classes or methods in isolation and this could include testing specific business logic items that are important to the business needs then the next approach is called integration testing now integration testing is a bit of a wide term but it essentially means that we test multiple components working together such as a controller service and repository it can also mean that we can test multiple parts of a wider system so the second part here basically means that we can test different microservices together with testing all of the microservices at once so for example this could be testing and API Gateway the off service and also the patient service without worrying about the analytics service the bilding service or the notification service so this is the test we're going to write as it replicates uh the manual approach we have been using so far so we'll see how this works in the next section but there's a few more tests that I want to make you aware of that are important to know as well so the first one is what's called end to end testing or E toe testing so this is the most complete level of testing where we would set up tools that will perform various workflows starting from the UI which would in the end call an API and also a database as well as different microservices as and when needed so this is essentially testing a system as the end user would use it so this is the most complete workflow but it also takes the most time to set up and can be the hardest to maintain and before we move on there's a few other tests that are worth mentioning as well so there is security testing which ensures that hackers can't access the internals of our system or the internals of our microservices there's also performance testing which tests how well our microservices set up performs under heavy loads and heavy traffic and there's contract testing which is testing the interfaces between microservices and we have acceptance testing which is testing that our functionality matches that of the business requirements that were specified for a given feature so that's a bit of an overview of some of the main testing approaches and as I mentioned we're going to use integration testing in this version of the course to automate the tests that we have been running manually so far so in here we have been using intellig rest clients to make a post request to login and this will go through the API Gateway to the off service and we will get a token back and then as a separate rest request from intell we were taking the token and we were passing it along with the request to get patients which also went through the API gateway to adapation service and and this also includes the middle step that validates the token and so on so each time we want to test this we have to do two steps manually and it becomes a bit cumbersome so this is what we are going to automate using a tool called rest assured and this is what we will do in the next section okay so now that we have an idea on the different types of testing that we can do we are going to jump in and GitHub just to make sure you compare your code to mine and catch any mistakes that could have popped up along the way okay so so far we have been running our containers locally using Docker this is fine for development however in Enterprise production environments we need a solution that is more scalable secure and reliable so this is where LS or Amazon web services comes into play so LS is a cloud provider that offers various tools and services to run our microservices in the cloud however LS incurs costs for running these Services which isn't ideal for us when we are trying to learn how production environments work and for veloping locally so even though LS has free tiers this is not equivalent to being free so just keep this in mind as it can sometimes catch people out um when they come to trying to use ls's free tier and then they see that there's a few dollars have been charge to their account so in order to get around this we're going to use a tool called local stack so local stack is a tool that allows us to run simulated reass environment locally on our development machines using Docker this allows us to develop and tests based applications in a safe way without incurring any of the costs that are associated with the real BS so even though the local stack environment is a simulated environment the code that we write to deploy our application to local stack is going to be fully compatible with the real alss so you're still getting the real hands-on experience that translates directly to production Enterprise environments that use Amazon web services but before we actually try and deploy our code and our microservices to the simulated ABS environment on local stack there's a few things that we need to learn about hows Works in order to host containers and run containers and all that stuff so there are a ton of services and Concepts to learn when it comes to abls and that would take a whole course in itself so for this course we are going to focus on the Bare Essentials that we need as Enterprise developers to create and run containers in the cloud so the first thing we need is what's called a VPC so a VPC C stands for a virtual private cloud and this is ANS provided resource that isolates other resources on a private Network and is secured from the outside world so a VPC also defines how the other services that we are going to use can communicate with each other so for example we are going to start a bunch of containers and start up databases and things like that and we need a way for these things to communicate to each other and this is what the VPC does so the VPC essentially creates a network and allows all the other services that we use to communicate with each other securely and protected from the outside world so the next thing that we need to know about is ECS so ECS stands for elastic container service and this is a container orchestration tool that runs and manages Docker containers ons so ECS is the Amazon service that we are going to be working with the most as this is what starts and runs all of our microservice containers so if if you are familiar with kubernetes which is another tool that orchestrates microservices this is very similar it's just AWS version of that so inside ECS then there's a number of things that we need to understand in order for us to implement ECS correctly so the first thing that we will need to create is an ECS cluster so a cluster is a logical group of ECS services and tasks that run within a VPC so once we've created our VPC we will create an ECS cluster and and all of our ECS services and tasks will go inside this ECS cluster so an ECS service then is in charge of managing an ECS task and also integrates with load balancers so for each of our microservices we're going to have an ECS service that manages the ECS task so an ECS task then is the actual running instance of a container which is Created from a task definition so for each of our microservice we are going to have an ECS task and this task will run uh the container itself so the task and the service can be a bit confusing to understand but the main thing to note is that the service is going to manage the task and the task manages the container and the last thing we need to know about is the task definition so this is a blueprint that defines how a container should run including its image its CPU its memory and any environment variables and any other settings that we need to do so the best way to think about a task definition is it's the same as the Run configurations that we created in intellig where we defined the container environment and variables and so on and this is what is used to create an actual container so we will see a diagram at the end of this section on how these bits and pieces fit together so don't worry if it doesn't make too much sense right now because there's a lot of different pieces to understand so the next thing that we need to know about is albs so an ALB stands for application load balancer and this is pretty important because since all of our infrastructure is secured inside a VPC we need a way for internet traffic such as requests from the front-end clients to reach our microservices and this is where the albs come in so albs are an ABS service that that lets us easily configure a load balancer so that traffic can access our VPC and access any of our ECS Services depending on how we set it up so the ALB um sits on the public subnet or in other words it's accessible by the internet and it forwards requests to the different e CS services and since our ECS services are inside of a VPC they are on the private subnet so a private subnet means that it can only be accessed specifically from the VPC itself so it cannot be accessed from the internet so in other words an ALB provides a secure entry point for all internet traffic such as our frontend clients so before we jump into the diagrams of our infrastructure as a quick reminder we are going to use Ms local stack so this allows us to run all the things that we have just talked about on our local Dev machines without incurring any costs that are associated with those services on ABS so local stack uses Docker so even though there's a few additional things to set up it's all going to run very similarly to how we have been running things so far using different Docker containers so there's a hobby tier that we can use that gives us access to all of the Ars Services that we need without having to pay for subscriptions which is very nice and it fits in to our current development workflow nicely as we will be using the same containers that we have been using in development so we'll see how this all works when we come to the coding bits of the section and unfortunately there are some features that are not supported uh or have limited support so these tend to be things around the security grips and the IM roles and things like that so so there are some there are some features that are not supported or have limited support such as security grips IM rules and Cloud Discovery and and service Discovery d to the limitations of trying to runs services on a single machine but don't worry this won't affect the course or any of the services we need it's just something to be aware of if you are experimenting on your own with some of the various services so just a quick look at local stack as you can see here we have a whole bunch of different ABS services that we can access and use to develop locally and if you happen to have access to an ABS account which you can sign up for yourself but you will need a credit or debit card you will see that these are the exact services that exist on the reals so we are going to be using a bunch of these to deploy our stack to as local stack environment okay so let's look and see how our microservices stack is going to fit into an LS infrastructure okay so the first thing that we have is we have the virtual private Cloud so this is the dark blue background here and this is rvpc so this is going to set up all the networking infrastructure that is needed for the different ABS services to communicate with each other and to make sure that each of these services are individually secure over here we have RDS which is our database setup and just below this we have msk which is our Kafka setup so these are the names of the managed services that LS provides to give us managed databases and to give us managed CFA so what I mean by managed is that LS handles the maintenance of our databases and our cafka cluster for us so so this means that we don't have to worry about database backups dependency upgrades or any of the infrastructure maintenance as EDS and msk will schedule and handle these things automatically for us so this is nice as as it means we don't have to worry about doing those things ourselves as they can be timec consuming and often tedious to perform especially in Enterprise environments so the downside of these services are that they incur a cost so since ARS is managing these services for us they're going to charge us in order to do that so even though these services are managed under the hood they are essentially the same things that we have been using on our local development setup using Docker so for development we have been using a postgress database for each of our services and we also started a local CFA instance using Docker as well and this is the exact same thing only managed bys and over here we have our ECS cluster so ECS is the container orchestration service that's going to run and manage all of our Docker containers and it provides a much more powerful flexible and scalable solution for production environments compared to the docker internal Network which we use locally during development so inside of our ECS cluster we have a bunch of ECS services so the ECS service is responsible for managing the ECS tasks and the ECS tasks are what actually run the containers so for example over here we have our off service and this is a container that is started by the ECS task and outside of the ECS task we have the ECS service that manages that task so the task runs the actual container it defines how much memory how much CPU the environment variables and so on that that containers needs to run and this is managed by the service and the service is going to manage the access to our task or our container which it can do through a load balancer or through ECS service Discovery the ECS service will also manage the scaling of the tasks so what I mean by that is is that if our off-service is receiving a lot of traffic the ECS service will automatically create a new task that will run another container so for example it could start as many containers as as we want or as we need depending on the traffic and in a similar fashion the ECS service will also handle if the container fails so if the ECS task stops because the container has stopped for some reason ECS service will see this and it will automatically start up a new container so this is why we need two different things that ultimately run a container so we can have as many services as we want inside of an ECS cluster and each of our microservices is going to have its own ECS Service as we can see here so we have our off service we have our billing service our Patient Service our API Gateway and we also have our analytics service down here and all these things are going to work the exact same way as they do in our local Dev environment so the API Gateway is going to receive requests it's going to check if those requests are valid and the patient service is going to send Kafka messages to our Kafka instance is over here in msk and the patient service is also going to send grpc requests to our billing service and then down here our analytic service is going to consume events from our Kafka topic so the functionality and infrastructure of our patient management setup is going to act the exact same as it will on our local Dev environment the only difference is that we are making it work ons using all the ABS services that it provides us so that's how ECS is going to work and the last thing we need to look at here is our application load balancer or our ALB so as a quick reminder in our local Dev setup we have set up a API Gateway that handles requests from the outside world and it validates the requests and forwards the request on to the respective microservice based on the rules that we Define so in our setup we are going to add a application load balancer to our infrastructure that will allow traffic to access the API Gateway from the internet so so the reason we need this is because all of our infrastructure is inside a VPC and this is secure from the internet so an application load balancer provides a way for our front-end clients to make requests through rvpc so we can then forward the requests onto any of our ECS services so in order to keep the functionality the same as we have seen on local Dev so far all our load balancer is going to do is it's going to forward the request to our API Gateway and from there our API Gateway is going to take over and it's going to act as it normally would so it would authenticate the request and it will forward the request on to the respective service depending on the rot so again functionally this is all the same we're just adding an extra step in front here to provide an entry point to our WS infrastructure for our front end clients and this is done in a secure way that we can completely control okay so that was a bit of a crash course ons and some of the most common services that you will see in Enterprise production environments and this is what we are going to build using local stack so hopefully you can see that this setup is very similar to how we run things locally the only difference is that we have a few extra layers and configurations to take advantage of's powerful container orchestration and infrastructure management and we are going to build these things out piece by piece so don't worry if it seems overwhelming at this point we're going to build it out piece by piece and we will check that each bit is working individually before we move on to the next bit and by the end you'll have a fully-fledged infrastructure that's running on your local machine that you can access uh to manage the patients so just a quick note on why do we need all this compl complexity when there are simpler deployment tools that exist so so if you haven't worked in an Enterprise environment before you're probably asking why do we need this level of complexity when there are simpler deployment tools and services that exist so for example there are tools that are great for deploying small full stack apps using a a few clicks but these tools have limitations for complex applications so we ask gives us full control over the infrastructure the scaling and the security of our infrastructure which makes it better for production environments so for example we have full control over ECS which lets us run the multiple microservices in containers we can decide how we want to scale those containers how much memory that we want to allocate to each service and to each task and we can also Define things like recovery and disaster management it also means that multiple teams can work on different areas without having to overlap so we might have a devops team who manages the infrastructure and we might have the feature teams who focus on building out patient management features and each of those teams can handle their own area independently of the other team and they have full control on how to do that we also have full control over things like RDS for our databases and cafka for our real time messaging and we can also control the access to our infrastructure using application load balancers and security rules and things like that so while there are tools out there that let us easily deploy simple code bases all of the infrastructure is hidden away from you and if you come up against a scenario where you need specific scalability or where you need specific infrastructure changes you will likely hit problems with these services so so this is why the largest Enterprise companies are using things like ABS as it gives them full control over their infrastructure okay so now the first thing that we're going to do is we're going to install the local stack application onto our updating infrastructure on the real BS we could just redeploy the new cloud formation stack but because local stack doesn't support that sort of functionality the local stack docs suggests that we try and delete the old one so if you do make changes to your stack just run the script again it will delete the old one and it will try and deploy the new one okay and the last thing to talk about is just how to troubleshoot some of this stuff so sometimes when you deploy some changes the stack get into a funny state where it won't delete and create either so you have a few options here you can go into the local stack desktop app and you can go into the container and go to the resource browser and go into cloud formation and from here you can choose the stack name you just take the box over here and under actions at the very bottom here you can choose remove selected so this will try and delete the stack and it will delete all the resources as well so if that fails to work for whatever reason the last thing you can try and do is on the containers is just try and delete this container and create a new one so that's the last line of defense option as it means you have to set up your API key and so on again which is not the end of the world but ideally you want to avoid that if you can but as a last result it should work fine as well that brings us to the end of this version of
